//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Increase2.svg/22px-Increase2.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="300" /> \xe2\x82\xac4.469 billion <small>(2018)</small></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Employees</th><td class="infobox-data">17,500 <small>(2019)</small><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Website</th><td class="infobox-data"><span class="url"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.ryanair.com">www<wbr />.ryanair<wbr />.com</a></span></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Ryanair</b> <a href="/wiki/Designated_activity_company" title="Designated activity company"><abbr title="Designated activity company">DAC</abbr></a><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> is an <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Irish</a> ultra <a href="/wiki/Low-cost_airline" class="mw-redirect" title="Low-cost airline">low-cost airline</a> founded in 1984. It is headquartered in <a href="/wiki/Swords,_Dublin" title="Swords, Dublin">Swords, Dublin</a>, with its primary operational bases at <a href="/wiki/Dublin_Airport" title="Dublin Airport">Dublin</a> and <a href="/wiki/London_Stansted_Airport" title="London Stansted Airport">London Stansted</a> airports. It forms the largest part of the Ryanair Holdings family of airlines, and has <a href="/wiki/Ryanair_UK" title="Ryanair UK">Ryanair UK</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buzz_(Ryanair)" title="Buzz (Ryanair)">Buzz</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Malta_Air" title="Malta Air">Malta Air</a> as sister airlines. In 2016, Ryanair was the largest European budget airline by scheduled passengers flown, and carried more international passengers than any other airline.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ryanair operates more than 400 <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_Next_Generation#737-800" title="Boeing 737 Next Generation">Boeing 737-800</a> aircraft, with a single <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_Next_Generation#737-700" title="Boeing 737 Next Generation">737-700</a> used as a charter aircraft, as a backup, and for pilot training.<sup id="cite_ref-fleet_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fleet-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The airline has been characterised by its rapid expansion, a result of the <a href="/wiki/Airline_deregulation" title="Airline deregulation">deregulation</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Aviation" title="Aviation">aviation industry</a> in Europe in 1997 and the success of its low-cost business model. Ryanair\'s route network serves 40 countries in Europe, North Africa (Morocco), and the Middle East (Israel, Lebanon and Jordan).<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The company has often been criticised for its poor working conditions,<sup id="cite_ref-Employee1_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Employee1-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Employee2_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Employee2-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Employee3_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Employee3-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> heavy use of extra charges,<sup id="cite_ref-Charges1_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Charges1-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Charges2_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Charges2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Charges4_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Charges4-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> bad customer service,<sup id="cite_ref-Which1_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Which1-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> and tendency to intentionally generate controversy in order to gain free publicity.<sup id="cite_ref-Stunt1_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stunt1-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stunt2_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stunt2-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ryanair&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Since its establishment in 1984,<sup id="cite_ref-founded_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-founded-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Ryanair has grown from a small airline, flying the short journey from <a href="/wiki/Waterford_Airport" title="Waterford Airport">Waterford</a> to London Gatwick, into Europe\'s largest carrier. There have been over 19,000 people working for the company, most employed and contracted by agencies to fly on Ryanair aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The airline went public in 1997,<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> the money raised was used to expand the airline into a pan-European carrier. <a href="/wiki/Revenue" title="Revenue">Revenues</a> have risen from <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">\xe2\x82\xac</a>231&#160;million in 1998 to \xe2\x82\xac1,843&#160;million in 2003 and to \xe2\x82\xac3,013&#160;million in 2010. Similarly, net profits have increased from \xe2\x82\xac48&#160;million to \xe2\x82\xac339&#160;million over the same period.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (January 2020)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ryanair&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ryanair_EMB-110.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Ryanair_EMB-110.jpg/220px-Ryanair_EMB-110.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Ryanair_EMB-110.jpg/330px-Ryanair_EMB-110.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Ryanair_EMB-110.jpg/440px-Ryanair_EMB-110.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="661" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ryanair_EMB-110.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ryanair <a href="/wiki/Embraer_EMB_110_Bandeirante" title="Embraer EMB 110 Bandeirante">Embraer EMB 110 Bandeirante</a> in 1988</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ryanair_ATR-42-300.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Ryanair_ATR-42-300.jpg/220px-Ryanair_ATR-42-300.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Ryanair_ATR-42-300.jpg/330px-Ryanair_ATR-42-300.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Ryanair_ATR-42-300.jpg/440px-Ryanair_ATR-42-300.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="723" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ryanair_ATR-42-300.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ryanair <a href="/wiki/ATR_42-300" class="mw-redirect" title="ATR 42-300">ATR 42-300</a> in 1991</div></div></div>\n<p>Ryanair was founded in 1984 as "Danren Enterprises"<sup id="cite_ref-founded_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-founded-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> by Christopher Ryan, Liam Lonergan (owner of Irish travel agent Club Travel), and Irish businessman <a href="/wiki/Tony_Ryan" title="Tony Ryan">Tony Ryan</a>, founder of <a href="/wiki/Guinness_Peat_Aviation" title="Guinness Peat Aviation">Guinness Peat Aviation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The airline was shortly renamed "Ryanair".<sup id="cite_ref-founded_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-founded-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> It began operations in 1985 flying a 15-seat <a href="/wiki/Embraer_EMB_110_Bandeirante" title="Embraer EMB 110 Bandeirante">Embraer Bandeirante</a> <a href="/wiki/Turboprop" title="Turboprop">turboprop</a> aircraft between <a href="/wiki/Waterford_Airport" title="Waterford Airport">Waterford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gatwick_Airport" title="Gatwick Airport">Gatwick Airport</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1986, the company added a second route\xe2\x80\x93flying Dublin to <a href="/wiki/Luton_Airport" title="Luton Airport">Luton</a>, thus directly competing with the <a href="/wiki/Aer_Lingus" title="Aer Lingus">Aer Lingus</a>/<a href="/wiki/British_Airways" title="British Airways">British Airways</a> duopoly for the first time. Under partial EEC deregulation, airlines could begin new international intra-EEC services, as long as one of the two governments gave approval (the so-called "double-disapproval" regime). The Irish government at the time refused its approval to protect Aer Lingus, but Britain\xe2\x80\x94under <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a>\'s deregulating <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a> government\xe2\x80\x94approved the service. With two routes and two aircraft, the fledgling airline carried 82,000 passengers in one year.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1986, the directors of Ryanair took an 85% stake in <a href="/wiki/London_European_Airways" title="London European Airways">London European Airways</a>. From 1987, this provided a connection with the Luton Ryanair service onward to Amsterdam and Brussels.<sup id="cite_ref-flight6_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flight6-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> In 1988, London European operated as Ryanair Europe and later began to operate charter services.<sup id="cite_ref-flight8_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flight8-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-flight9_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flight9-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> That same year, <a href="/wiki/Michael_O%27Leary_(Ryanair)" class="mw-redirect" title="Michael O&#39;Leary (Ryanair)">Michael O\'Leary</a> joined the company as chief financial officer.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> In 1989, a <a href="/wiki/Short_Sandringham" title="Short Sandringham">Short Sandringham</a> was operated with Ryanair sponsorship titles but never flew revenue-generating services for the airline.<sup id="cite_ref-Simper_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Simper-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Due to decreasing profits, the company restructured in 1990, copying the low fares model of <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Airlines" title="Southwest Airlines">Southwest Airlines</a>, after O\'Leary visited the company.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="1992.E2.80.932009"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1992\xe2\x80\x932009">1992\xe2\x80\x932009</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ryanair&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1992\xe2\x80\x932009">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ryanair_BAC_111_EI-BVI.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Ryanair_BAC_111_EI-BVI.jpg/220px-Ryanair_BAC_111_EI-BVI.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Ryanair_BAC_111_EI-BVI.jpg/330px-Ryanair_BAC_111_EI-BVI.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Ryanair_BAC_111_EI-BVI.jpg/440px-Ryanair_BAC_111_EI-BVI.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1050" data-file-height="703" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ryanair_BAC_111_EI-BVI.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ryanair operated <a href="/wiki/BAC_1-11" class="mw-redirect" title="BAC 1-11">BAC 1-11</a> series 500 aircraft between 1988 and 1993</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ryanair_737-200_EI-CKS.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Ryanair_737-200_EI-CKS.jpg/220px-Ryanair_737-200_EI-CKS.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Ryanair_737-200_EI-CKS.jpg/330px-Ryanair_737-200_EI-CKS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Ryanair_737-200_EI-CKS.jpg/440px-Ryanair_737-200_EI-CKS.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="695" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ryanair_737-200_EI-CKS.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ryanair <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737-200" class="mw-redirect" title="Boeing 737-200">Boeing 737-200</a> in 2003</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ryanair_Boeing_737-800_in_SXF.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ryanair_Boeing_737-800_in_SXF.jpg/220px-Ryanair_Boeing_737-800_in_SXF.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="87" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ryanair_Boeing_737-800_in_SXF.jpg/330px-Ryanair_Boeing_737-800_in_SXF.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ryanair_Boeing_737-800_in_SXF.jpg/440px-Ryanair_Boeing_737-800_in_SXF.jpg 2x" data-file-width="880" data-file-height="346" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ryanair_Boeing_737-800_in_SXF.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ryanair <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737-800" class="mw-redirect" title="Boeing 737-800">Boeing 737-800</a> in a former livery</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1992, the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>\'s deregulation of the air industry in Europe gave carriers from one EU country the right to operate scheduled services between other EU states and represented a major opportunity for Ryanair.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> After a successful flotation on the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Stock_Exchange" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Stock Exchange">Dublin</a> and the <a href="/wiki/NASDAQ" class="mw-redirect" title="NASDAQ">NASDAQ</a> stock exchanges, the airline launched services to <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sandefjord_Airport,_Torp" title="Sandefjord Airport, Torp">Sandefjord Airport, Torp</a> (110&#160;km south of Oslo), <a href="/wiki/Beauvais%E2%80%93Till%C3%A9_Airport" title="Beauvais\xe2\x80\x93Till\xc3\xa9 Airport">Beauvais\xe2\x80\x93Till\xc3\xa9</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charleroi" title="Charleroi">Charleroi</a> near <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> In 1998, flush with new capital, the airline placed a massive US$2 <a href="/wiki/1000000000_(number)" class="mw-redirect" title="1000000000 (number)">billion</a> order for 45 new <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737#737-800" title="Boeing 737">Boeing 737-800</a> series aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The airline launched its website in 2000, with online booking initially said to be a small and unimportant part of the software supporting the site. Increasingly the online booking contributed to the aim of cutting flight prices by selling directly to passengers and excluding the costs imposed by travel agents. Within a year, the website was handling three-quarters of all bookings.\n</p><p>Ryanair launched a new base of operation in <a href="/wiki/Charleroi_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Charleroi Airport">Charleroi Airport</a> in 2001. Later that year, the airline ordered 155 new <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737-800" class="mw-redirect" title="Boeing 737-800">737-800</a> aircraft from <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">Boeing</a> at what was believed to be a substantial discount, to be delivered over eight years from 2002 to 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Approximately 100 of these aircraft had been delivered by the end of 2005, although there were slight delays in late 2005 caused by production disruptions arising from a Boeing machinists\' strike.<sup id="cite_ref-IrishTimes2005_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IrishTimes2005-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In April 2003, Ryanair acquired its ailing competitor <a href="/wiki/Buzz_(airline)" title="Buzz (airline)">Buzz</a> from <a href="/wiki/KLM" title="KLM">KLM</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IrishTimes2003_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IrishTimes2003-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During 2004, Michael O\'Leary warned of a "bloodbath" during the winter from which only two or three low-cost airlines would emerge, the expectation being that these would be Ryanair and <a href="/wiki/EasyJet" title="EasyJet">EasyJet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IrishTimes2004_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IrishTimes2004-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> A loss of \xe2\x82\xac3.3&#160;million in the second quarter of 2004 was the airline\'s first recorded loss for 15 years but the airline became profitable soon after. The <a href="/wiki/Enlargement_of_the_European_Union" title="Enlargement of the European Union">enlargement of the European Union</a> on 1 May 2004 opened the way to more new routes for Ryanair.<sup id="cite_ref-IrishTimes2005b_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IrishTimes2005b-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The rapid addition of new routes and new bases has enabled growth in passenger numbers and made Ryanair among the largest carriers on European routes. In August 2005, the airline claimed to have carried 20% more passengers within Europe than British Airways.<sup id="cite_ref-about_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-about-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For the six months ending on September 30, 2006 passenger traffic grew by more than a fifth to 22.1 million passengers and revenues rose by a third to \xe2\x82\xac1.256 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 13 February 2006, Britain\'s <a href="/wiki/Channel_4" title="Channel 4">Channel 4</a> broadcast a documentary as part of its <a href="/wiki/Dispatches_(TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="Dispatches (TV series)"><i>Dispatches</i></a> series, "Ryanair caught napping". The documentary criticised Ryanair\'s training policies, security procedures and aircraft hygiene, and highlighted poor staff morale. Ryanair denied the allegations<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> and claimed that promotional materials, in particular a photograph of a stewardess sleeping, had been faked by <i>Dispatches</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 5 October 2006, Ryanair launched a \xe2\x82\xac1.48 billion (\xc2\xa31 billion; $1.9 billion) bid to buy fellow Irish carrier <a href="/wiki/Aer_Lingus" title="Aer Lingus">Aer Lingus</a>. On 2 October 2006, Aer Lingus rejected Ryanair\'s takeover bid, saying it was contradictory.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In August 2006, the company started charging passengers to check-in at the airport, therefore reversing its policy of paying for online check-in. It says that by cutting airport check-in, it reduces overhead costs.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ryanair\'s CEO, Michael O\'Leary, stated in April 2007 that Ryanair planned to launch a new <a href="/wiki/Long-haul" class="mw-redirect" title="Long-haul">long-haul</a> airline around 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The new airline would be separate from Ryanair and operate under different branding. It would offer both low costs with fares starting at \xe2\x82\xac10.00 and a business class service which would be much more expensive, intended to rival airlines like <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Atlantic" title="Virgin Atlantic">Virgin Atlantic</a>. The new airline would operate from Ryanair\'s existing bases in Europe to approximately six new bases in the United States. The new American bases will not be main bases such as New York\'s <a href="/wiki/JFK_airport" class="mw-redirect" title="JFK airport">JFK airport</a>, but smaller airports located outside major cities. Since the Boeing 787 was sold out of production until at least 2012, and the Airbus A350 XWB will not enter service until 2014, this has contributed a delay to the airline\'s launch. It is said that the name of the new airline will be RyanAtlantic and it will sell tickets through the Ryanair website under an alliance agreement.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In February 2010, O\'Leary said the launch would be delayed until 2014, at the earliest, because of the shortage of suitable, cheap aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In October 2008, Ryanair withdrew operations from a base in Europe for the first time when it closed its base in Valencia, Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Ryanair estimated the closure cost 750 jobs.<sup id="cite_ref-closevlc_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-closevlc-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 1 December 2008, Ryanair launched a second takeover bid of Aer Lingus, offering an all-cash offer of \xe2\x82\xac748 million (<a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">\xc2\xa3</a>619 mil; US$950 million). The offer was a 28% premium on the value of Aer Lingus stock, during the preceding 30 days. Ryanair said, "Aer Lingus, as a small, stand-alone, regional airline, has been marginalised and bypassed, as most other EU flag carriers consolidate." The two airlines would operate separately. Ryanair stated it would double the Aer Lingus short-haul fleet from 33 to 66 and create 1,000 new jobs.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The Aer Lingus board rejected the offer and advised its <a href="/wiki/Shareholder" title="Shareholder">shareholders</a> to take no action.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> On 22 January 2009, Ryanair walked away from the Aer Lingus takeover bid after it was rejected by the Irish government on the grounds it undervalued the airline and would harm competition.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> However, Ryanair retained a stake in Aer Lingus; in October 2010, competition regulators in the UK opened an inquiry, due to concerns that Ryanair\'s stake may lead to a reduction in competition.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2009, Ryanair announced that it was in talks with Boeing and <a href="/wiki/Airbus" title="Airbus">Airbus</a> about an order that could include up to 200 aircraft. Even though Ryanair had dealt with Boeing aircraft up to that point, Michael O\'Leary said he would buy Airbus aircraft if it offered a better deal. Airbus Chief Commercial Officer <a href="/wiki/John_Leahy_(executive)" title="John Leahy (executive)">John Leahy</a> denied in February 2009 that any negotiations were taking place.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 21 February 2009, Ryanair confirmed it was planning to close all <a href="/wiki/Airport_check-in" title="Airport check-in">check-in desks</a> by the start of 2010. Michael O\'Leary, Ryanair\'s chief executive, said passengers would be able to leave their luggage at a bag drop, but everything else would be done online. This became reality in October 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In June 2009, Ryanair reported its first annual loss, with a loss posted of \xe2\x82\xac169 million for the financial year ending 31 March.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In November 2009, Ryanair announced that negotiations with Boeing had proceeded poorly and that Ryanair was thinking of stopping the negotiations, then put at 200 aircraft for delivery between 2013 and 2016, and simply returning cash to shareholders.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Boeing\'s competitor Airbus was mentioned again as an alternative vendor for Ryanair, but both Michael O\'Leary and Airbus CCO John Leahy dismissed this.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> In December 2009, Ryanair confirmed that negotiations with Boeing had indeed failed. Plans were to take all 112 aircraft already on order at that point, with the last deliveries occurring in 2012, for a total fleet of over 300. Ryanair confirmed that an agreement had been met on price, but it had failed to agree on conditions, as Ryanair had wanted to carry forward certain conditions from its previous contract.<sup id="cite_ref-ryanair181209_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ryanair181209-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2010s">2010s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ryanair&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 2010s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ryanair_B737-800_Cabin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" href="/wiki/Glasgow_Airport" title="Glasgow Airport">Glasgow International Airport</a>, Scotland</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Ryanair_Abflughalle_at_Bremen_Airport_001.JPG/330px-Ryanair_Abflughalle_at_Bremen_Airport_001.JPG 1.5x, href="/wiki/Bremen_Airport" title="Bremen Airport">Bremen Airport</a>, Germany</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a data-file-height="2875" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ryanair_Boeing_737-800_EI-DAK_2_(27632982163).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ryanair maintenance hangars at <a href="/wiki/London_Stansted_Airport" title="London Stansted Airport">London Stansted Airport</a>, England</div></div></div>\n<p>In April 2010, after <a href="/wiki/Air_travel_disruption_after_the_2010_Eyjafjallaj%C3%B6kull_eruption" title="Air travel disruption after the 2010 Eyjafjallaj\xc3\xb6kull eruption">a week of flight disruption</a> in Europe caused by the <a href="/wiki/2010_eruptions_of_Eyjafjallaj%C3%B6kull" title="2010 eruptions of Eyjafjallaj\xc3\xb6kull">2010 eruptions of Eyjafjallaj\xc3\xb6kull</a> in Iceland, Ryanair decided to end refusals to comply with EU regulations which stated it was obliged to reimburse stranded passengers.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> In a company statement released on 22 April 2010, Ryanair described the regulations as \'unfair\'. On 29 April 2010, Ryanair cancelled all of its routes from <a href="/wiki/Liszt_Ferenc_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Liszt Ferenc Airport">Budapest Liszt Ferenc Airport</a> after talks with the airport\'s management on reducing fees failed. As the airport is the only one serving <a href="/wiki/Budapest" title="Budapest">Budapest</a>, there is no lower-cost airport nearby. In June 2010, Ryanair called for the Irish government to scrap its tourist tax, implying it was destroying tourism in Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> In August 2010, Ryanair held a press conference in <a href="/wiki/Plovdiv" title="Plovdiv">Plovdiv</a> and announced its first ever Bulgarian destination connecting Plovdiv with London Stansted. The service was planned to start in November 2010 with two flights weekly.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> In late 2010, Ryanair began withdrawing all routes from its smallest base, Belfast City, and Shannon due to increased airport fees.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the last three months of 2010, Ryanair made a loss of \xe2\x82\xac10.3 million, compared with a loss of \xe2\x82\xac10.9 million in the same period the previous year. More than 3,000 flights were cancelled in the quarter. Ryanair blamed the losses on strikes and flight cancellations due to severe weather.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In March 2011, Ryanair opened a new maintenance hangar at Glasgow Prestwick International Airport, making it Ryanair\'s biggest fleet maintenance base. In June 2011, Ryanair and <a href="/wiki/COMAC" class="mw-redirect" title="COMAC">COMAC</a> signed an agreement to co-operate on the development of the <a href="/wiki/C-919" class="mw-redirect" title="C-919">C-919</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737" title="Boeing 737">Boeing 737</a> competitor.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ryanair cut capacity by grounding 80 aircraft between November 2011 and April 2012 due to the high cost of fuel and continuing weak economic conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 19 June 2012, Ryanair Chief Executive Michael O\'Leary announced his intention to make an all-cash offer for Aer Lingus. The bid was blocked by the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a> in 2017, which had also blocked an earlier bid.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to research in October 2013 Ryanair was the cheapest low-cost airline in Europe in basic price (excluding fees) but was the fourth cheapest when fees were included.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 25 October 2013, Ryanair announced what it described as a series of "customer service improvements", to take place over the next six months. These included lower fees for reprinting boarding passes, free changes of minor errors on bookings within 24 hours, and a free second small carry on bag. Ryanair said it was making the changes as a result of customer feedback.<sup id="cite_ref-CS_Imprivements_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CS_Imprivements-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 27 January 2014, Ryanair moved into a new \xe2\x82\xac20m, 100,000 sq ft Dublin head office in Airside Business Park, having outgrown its previous office within Dublin Airport.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The building was officially opened on Thursday 3 April 2014 by Taoiseach <a href="/wiki/Enda_Kenny" title="Enda Kenny">Enda Kenny</a>, Minister for Finance <a href="/wiki/Michael_Noonan_(Fine_Gael_politician)" title="Michael Noonan (Fine Gael politician)">Michael Noonan</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Lord_Mayor_of_Dublin" title="Lord Mayor of Dublin">Lord Mayor of Dublin</a> <a href="/wiki/Oisin_Quinn" class="mw-redirect" title="Oisin Quinn">Oisin Quinn</a>.\n</p><p>On 8 September 2014, Ryanair agreed to purchase up to 200 <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_MAX_8" class="mw-redirect" title="Boeing 737 MAX 8">Boeing 737 MAX 8s</a> (100 confirmed and 100 options) for over $22 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-737max_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-737max-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The airline confirmed plans to open an operating base at <a href="/wiki/Milan_Malpensa_Airport" title="Milan Malpensa Airport">Milan Malpensa Airport</a> from December 2015, initially with one aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 9 March 2016, Ryanair launched a corporate jet charter service, offering a Boeing 737-700 for corporate or group hire.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In November 2016, Ryanair launched a new package holiday service named Ryanair Holidays. The new service offers flights, accommodation, and transfer packages. The service was launched in Ireland, the United Kingdom and Germany, with other markets to follow.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Ryanair partnered with Spain-based tour operator, Logitravel, and accommodation provider, World2Meet, to create Ryanair Holidays.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In April 2017, Ryanair started issuing tickets for connecting flights, meaning if a connection is missed, the customer will be rebooked at no extra cost and compensated according to the <a href="/wiki/Flight_Compensation_Regulation_261/2004" class="mw-redirect" title="Flight Compensation Regulation 261/2004">EU Flight Compensation Regulation</a>. To begin with, such tickets were only sold for flights with connections at Rome-Fiumicino airport.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2017, the company announced plans to add 50 new aircraft to its fleet every year for the next five years, aiming to reach 160 million passengers by the early 2020s, up from 120 million passengers.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Abandoning_the_single-airline_strategy">Abandoning the single-airline strategy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ryanair&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Abandoning the single-airline strategy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>For over a decade, Ryanair had only operated with its Irish Air Operator\'s Certificate and solely under the Ryanair brand. However, starting in 2018 the airline began introducing additional brands and operating on multiple certificates in different countries.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SP-RSA_(44724544240).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/SP-RSA_%2844724544240%29.jpg/220px-SP-RSA_%2844724544240%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="90" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/SP-RSA_%2844724544240%29.jpg/330px-SP-RSA_%2844724544240%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/SP-RSA_%2844724544240%29.jpg/440px-SP-RSA_%2844724544240%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2230" data-file-height="908" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SP-RSA_(44724544240).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Boeing 737 operated by Polish Ryanair Sun subsidiary, recognizable only through Polish aircraft registration</div></div></div>\n<p>In 2017, Ryanair announced that it would launch an independent Polish subsidiary in 2018, operating charter flights from Poland to Mediterranean destinations. Aside from turning away from the company\'s policy of only operating on a single Air Operator\'s Certificate, the step also meant that Ryanair would be launching charter flights after having focused only on scheduled operations before.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> The subsidiary was branded <a href="/wiki/Ryanair_Sun" class="mw-redirect" title="Ryanair Sun">Ryanair Sun</a> and received its Polish Air Operator\'s Certificate in April 2018 and subsequently launched<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Initially, it had only one former Ryanair Boeing 737-800 and complemented its operation with wet-leased aircraft from its mother company. In late 2018, Ryanair Sun was expanded by transferring all Polish-based Ryanair aircraft to it. The decision was made in the wake of staff costs and unions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="&quot;Staff costs and unions&quot; where? (October 2019)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> As a consequence, Ryanair Sun mainly operated scheduled flights on behalf of its mother company using Ryanair\'s FR flight numbers.\n</p><p>Also in 2018, Ryanair expanded its portfolio with Austrian-based <a href="/wiki/Laudamotion" class="mw-redirect" title="Laudamotion">Laudamotion</a>, later renamed to "Lauda". Laudamotion was the successor of <a href="/wiki/Niki_(airline)" title="Niki (airline)">Niki</a>, which had folded as a consequence of the <a href="/wiki/Air_Berlin" title="Air Berlin">Air Berlin</a> demise.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The company was founded by <a href="/wiki/Niki_Lauda" title="Niki Lauda">Niki Lauda</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Initially, Ryanair purchased a 25-percent share in Laudamotion with the intention to increase the share to 75 percent pending government approval. The deal was announced in March 2018 ahead of the carrier\'s launch in June 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> After increasing its share to 75 percent, Ryanair fully acquired the Austrian airline in December 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 28 September 2018, pilots, cabin crew and other staff called for a strike due to the transition from workers being employed on Irish contracts and subject to Irish legislation to their own countries\' labour laws, along with an issue in their pay. Due to the lobbying of the crew and walk-outs of pilots, the airline had to cancel 250 flights, which affected around 40,000 passengers.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 9 June 2019, Ryanair announced (together with the Government of Malta), that it would establish a new airline called <a href="/wiki/Malta_Air" title="Malta Air">Malta Air</a>, which will consist of an initial fleet of ten aircraft and assume the 61 flights currently operated by Ryanair from the island. The fleet was registered in Malta while a new repair and maintenance hangar was also set up.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Ryanair transferred all its existing Maltese operations to the new airline and its fleet was expected to increase from the six Boeing 737-800 aircraft currently allocated to the Malta market to ten (all to be in Malta Air colours) by mid-2020.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2020s">2020s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ryanair&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 2020s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The carrier\'s CEO made comments at the A4E Aviation Summit in Brussels on 3 March 2020. Michael O\'Leary said that he expected people to get \'bored\' of the COVID-19 pandemic and saw a recovery by summer 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> That changed, with Ryanair announcing in a statement that it expected demand to return to 2019 levels by the summer of 2022.<sup id="cite_ref-aeronewsx.com_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aeronewsx.com-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic" title="COVID-19 pandemic">COVID-19 pandemic</a> has had a significant impact on Ryanair. While the CEO, Michael O\'Leary, remains adamant that state aid is not an option, the carrier has announced a number of changes to its operations. This includes the loss of 3000 jobs, announced on 1 May 2020, which will affect mainly pilots and cabin crew. This comes as the airline announced it would suspend the majority of its operations until June 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-aeronewsx.com_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aeronewsx.com-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> In July 2020, Ryanair\'s CEO, Michael O\'Leary announced that the company had made a net loss of \xe2\x82\xac185 million in the period April\xe2\x80\x93June 2020. In comparison, in the same period last year, the firm made a net profit of \xe2\x82\xac243 million.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> In September 2020, the airline threatened to leave Ireland due to COVID-19 restrictions.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Despite their original plan, to fly 60 per cent of the previous year\'s schedule, in October 2020, the company decided to reduce the number of flights between the period of November 2020\xe2\x80\x93March 2021 to 40 per cent. According to O\'Leary, this was a result of "government mismanagement of EU air travel" as the quarantine travel measures were loosened.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> By the end of December 2020, the airline reported an 83 per cent drop in annual passengers, from 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Due to the persisting COVID-19 pandemic, Ryanair is expecting losses of between \xe2\x82\xac800m and \xe2\x82\xac850m in their financial year of 2021. Only 27.5 million passengers flew compared to 148.6 million passengers in the previous financial year. The full financial report is due to be released on 17 May 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Corporate_affairs">Corporate affairs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ryanair&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Corporate affairs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Business_trends">Business trends</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ryanair&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Business trends">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The key trends for the Ryanair Group since 2010 are (as at year ending 31 March):\n</p>\n<center>\n<table class="wikitable">\n\n<tbody><tr href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</center>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Head_office">Head office</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ryanair&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Head office">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ryanair_HQ.jpg" class="image"><img alt=""
